<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8538b5a8-2b2a-4fd4-b524-31add90b0cd0'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<div id="review_1773501022" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1773501022" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1773501022?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401230432" href="/book/show/8941959-a-game-of-you">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=weird-fiction">weird-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1773501022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9298681523554874599"><br/>I have read the first five volumes of <i>The Sandman</i>, and so far this is only my third favorite (after <i>A Doll's House</i> and <i>Seasons of Mist</i>). Its development is somewhat muddy, its narrative too crowded with characters, and it is somewhat removed from the central myth of Sandman and the theological and cosmic questions which surround him, themes I find the most compelling part of this series. <br/><br/>I admit, though, that this preference may be merely a matter of taste. What <i>A Game of You</i> lacks in abstract m</span>
  <span id="freeText9298681523554874599" style="display:none"><br />I have read the first five volumes of <i>The Sandman</i>, and so far this is only my third favorite (after <i>A Doll's House</i> and <i>Seasons of Mist</i>). Its development is somewhat muddy, its narrative too crowded with characters, and it is somewhat removed from the central myth of Sandman and the theological and cosmic questions which surround him, themes I find the most compelling part of this series. <br /><br />I admit, though, that this preference may be merely a matter of taste. What <i>A Game of You</i> lacks in abstract matters, it compensates for in its emphasis on the personal. As the title indicates, this tale is about human psychology and the stratagems we employ both to hide from ourselves and to endure what we must. But it is also more than mere psychologizing, for it is metaphysical too, suggesting that our dreams may be rooted in an extra-personal reality, a pre-planned landscape which can be invaded and dominated by others. <br /><br />Our tale opens as the sleeping Barbie--a character (along with her husband Ken) from "A Doll's House"--is being summoned by a group of little talking animals to a dream world where she is the Princess, and in which her friends the animals are being menaced by someone--or somehing-- called "The Cuckoo." Soon The Cuckoo extends its menace to the waking world, Barbie is sucked into the world of dreams, and her fellow apartment dwellers--a lesbian couple (one of whom looks like an adorably benign Pete Lorre), a transgender woman, and a hippie witch (who is a lot--I mean a <i>lot</i>--older than she looks.) Their quest to defeat the cuckoo, and the help they receive from Dream, occupies most of the narrative.<br /><br />Perhaps most remarkable about this tale is its great range, from the childlike and whimsical to the violent and horrific. Nothing in this tale--neither the sentiment nor the ghastly humor--seems the least bit out of place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9298681523554874599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1018138021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1018138021" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1018138021?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401230432" href="/book/show/8941959-a-game-of-you">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=comic-book">comic-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1018138021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10724865586162498078"><b>
  <u>We’re back in the game!!!</u>
</b><br/><br/><br/><u>Creative Team:</u><br/><br/>Writer: Neil Gaiman<br/><br/>Illustrators: Shawn McManus, Colleen Doran, Bryan Talbot, George Pratt & Stan Woch<br/><br/>Covers: Dave McKean<br/><br/>Letterer: Todd Klein<br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>FAIRY TALES, NATURE’S FORCES, BLOOdY WITCHCRAFT & COMPACT’S TERMS</u>
</b><br/><br/>I am truly <b>GLAD</b> that my struggles in <i>Season of Mists</i> (the fourth volume) were left there and Neil Gaiman played with my mind a game, a wonderful game of words and images, a game where ironically you are given the rules of it until the end, but that</span>
  <span id="freeText10724865586162498078" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>We’re back in the game!!!</u>
</b><br /><br /><br /><u>Creative Team:</u><br /><br />Writer: Neil Gaiman<br /><br />Illustrators: Shawn McManus, Colleen Doran, Bryan Talbot, George Pratt &amp; Stan Woch<br /><br />Covers: Dave McKean<br /><br />Letterer: Todd Klein<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>FAIRY TALES, NATURE’S FORCES, BLOOdY WITCHCRAFT &amp; COMPACT’S TERMS</u>
</b><br /><br />I am truly <b>GLAD</b> that my struggles in <i>Season of Mists</i> (the fourth volume) were left there and Neil Gaiman played with my mind a game, a wonderful game of words and images, a game where ironically you are given the rules of it until the end, but that isn’t a reason for not enjoying the fullest this remarkable game of dreaming awake while reading, a game that it is not for the faint of heart.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Everybody dreams. I read about it. You don’t dream, you go koo-koo.</i>
</b><br /><br />What is a Dream-King without dreamers?<br /><br />What is Death without living people?<br /><br />Morpheus and Death are powerful, they are members of the Endless, however not matter how powerful they are, they still need people.<br /><br />Without people, their realms are left without purpose, without content, without richness.<br /><br />We aren’t islands and we shouldn’t wish to be ones.<br /><br />While you read more and more <i>The Sandman</i>, you realice that while Morpheus, the Dream-King, is indeed the title character, the story gets its impact thanks to the recurrent characters, that even you don’t realize that they will be recurrent until you meet them again. While some first encounters with the “secondary” characters give you a clear message that they are relevant, sometimes, they will just make a cameo or a brief appearance, but doing something vital in a following storyarc. <br /><br />Nothing happens by chance in <i>The Sandman</i>, each event has a purpose, a content, a richness.<br /><br />But not always each event will be blissful, some will bring sorrow, but such is life.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Thessaly? What’s going on here?</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Just stuff.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Stuff?</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Uh huh.</i>
</b><br /><br />While the storyarc has many artists involved, I want to highlight the sections illustrated by Shawn McManus, which I think was a perfect choice due the topic in this particular storyarc.<br /><br />If you already read <i>The Doll’s House</i> (the second volume) you already met stuff hard to digest, and here? Oh, baby, you will met more stuff kinda harder to digest. Some truly sad provoking you to... cry a little, and some truly gross provoking you to... check your door’s locks.<br /><br /><i>A Game of You</i> is Neil Gaiman’s tribute to all those epic fantasies and fairy tales, such as <i>Alice in Wonderland</i>, <i>The Chronicles of Narnia</i> and <i>Wizard of Oz</i>, as long as a tribute to all those games that we played in our childhoods, such as “Hide-and-Seek”, “Simon Says”, “Puzzles”, “Card Games”, “Playing House”, etc...<br /><br /><b>
  <i>But she still has to be stopped.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Why?</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Well, she’s dangerous. She’s evil.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Dangerous? Perhaps. But Evil? She acts according to her nature. Is that evil?</i>
</b><br /><br />We meet again Barbie, one of the characters introduced in <i>The Doll’s House</i>, she is now living in New York, in an apartment building which its owner is out of town, but she has plenty company with hr neighbors: Hazel and Foxglove (introduced (at least from my perspective) in <i>Death: The High Cost of Living</i> and visited again in <i>Death: The Time of your Life</i>, but here you will have them in a moment of the past), Thessaly (a nerdy/geeky-look-like petite woman with huge spectacles which is in reality a lot more than what she appears to be (I loved her character!!! I hope to meet her again in following stories)), George (mysterious quite man which, yes, you guess right, he is more than he appears to be, of course he will! This is <i>The Sandman</i>!!!) and Wanda (best friend of Barbie and born as Alvin Robert Caleb Mann, but in the process to change that).<br /><br />Barbie isn’t dreaming, and soon enough she will realize that she should be thankful of that since when she would dream again, nobody will be safe! Not matter if they are in or out of her dreams!<br /><br />This is such great creative story telling us that not always taking an action is the right choice, sometimes the best way to help somebody is not doing anything, but of course, we aren’t gods for not saying Endless members to know when to act and when not, as in anything in our lives and our interaction with others, we just can take responsibility for our actions trusting that our choices are for the better.<br /><br />Also, this story is about knowing ourselves and embrace each part of us, even if we want to make a dramatic change in our existences, we can’t forget each element that makes what we are, since each tiny little piece, not matter if we like them or not, not matter if we think that we would be better if we forget them, each portion, visible or invisible, solid or intangible, all they are components of ourselves, and each fragment is part of us for a reason, a purpose, adding content, adding richness.<br /><br />Don’t stop playing the game, the game of life until it would be over, and maybe, ironically, somebody, maybe a smiling girl in goth clothes, would tell us the rules of the game, the game that we played. And remember, you don’t decide when the game is over, it’s a call for the game itself. You just have to play it as best as you can, to the fullest, with all the power of your will.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10724865586162498078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2092465665" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2092465665" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2092465665?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401230432" href="/book/show/8941959-a-game-of-you">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=comic-graphic-novel">comic-graphic-novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2092465665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8908356212580003004"><b>The spell has been broken, the dream has ended. Well, at least, for now. </b><br/><br/>I was really surprised at how weak the writing was in this volume; it felt more like a spin-off than part of the actual series. Dream was barely present; he was more of a side character, only appearing briefly in the series that was named after him. Whatever was Neil Gaiman thinking when he decided to centre the story on Barbie?<br/><br/>Yes, Barbie. If felt so off centre and removed from The Sandman myth: the very thing that makes t</span>
  <span id="freeText8908356212580003004" style="display:none"><b>The spell has been broken, the dream has ended. Well, at least, for now. </b><br /><br />I was really surprised at how weak the writing was in this volume; it felt more like a spin-off than part of the actual series. Dream was barely present; he was more of a side character, only appearing briefly in the series that was named after him. Whatever was Neil Gaiman thinking when he decided to centre the story on Barbie?<br /><br />Yes, Barbie. If felt so off centre and removed from The Sandman myth: the very thing that makes this series so compelling, intelligent and cool. Sure it had lots of twisted elements and horror tropes but it still just didn’t feel right. The last edition was about Lucifer. Previous ones have been about crazy serial killers with teeth for eyes and all manner of fucked up things. We’ve even had an edition on the endless. So this felt rather (how shall I put it?) unimpressive and unimaginative when you compare it with what came before. The problem with such a great series as this is that when it fails to deliver, it really fails to deliver. <br /><br />Moreover, the artwork changed after two issues. The characters had completely different faces. I had to go back and check who was who based upon the clothing and hair styles; it was quite odd. Perhaps this is just a problem with my edition? The only character who had any sense of presence remains an enigma. She clearly had some past with Dream, though it is never divulged to us. The Witch was interesting; this is clearly the last we will ever see her however. All in all, it has far too much Barbie and not enough Dream. <br /><br />As such, I’m going to be taking a considerable break from this series. I do love it, but I need time before going back. It won’t be until early next year before I pick up another volume, I was just so disappointed here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8908356212580003004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText686467379488678459" style="display:none">Re-Read 5/1/20:<br /><br />*shiver* Such goodness may deserve a more verbose review, but fortunately, I already wrote one. :) And I don't really have much new to say. :)<br /><br />Original Review:<br /><br />I love Barbie. I love Wanda. I love Thessaly.<br /><br />Truly, this was one hell of a tightly-woven story including inner-worlds, cuckoo birds, ancient witches, pulling down the moon, and death.<br /><br />There's no way in hell that I could really boil it down to essentials. As a whole, it seriously rocks and hits me in the feels. Sexual identity and childhood and babies are only a part of it. Being wise and forgiving is only a part of it.<br /><br />Hell, I see that holy-bitch at Wanda's funeral and I see her just acting in her nature, just like the Cuckoo, and I feel like Thessaly, knowing I really ought to behave like Barbie. It tears me up.<br /><br />This is serious literature. Both fantastic, strange, and deep as hell and sharp. :) Hard to believe, after reading it, that it is a comic.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer856773534388323661">I really like how Gaiman explores child imagined fantasy worlds like Narnia or Oz. Yet at the same time this is also very much about identity and how we view ourselves, especially those of queer and trans orientations. Yet at the same time this is also very much about identity and how we view ourselves, especially those of queer and trans orientations. I really enjoy how Gaiman weaves in elements of past storylines as this reaches back to minor characters from <i>A Doll's House</i> and the demented issue where Dr. Destiny tortures the patrons of a diner in <i>Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</i>. These little Easter eggs really add to my enjoyment of the stories when I see them. I like Shawn McManus's cartoony art and feel it fits this story perfectly.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126262-brooke?shelf=graphic-novels-read">graphic-novels-read</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63310087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10944659504507569425">A Game of You, the 5th volume in the Sandman series, is my favorite so far. One thing I've noticed Gaiman is very good at is picking up little threads he dropped in previous stories and building on them (this was one of my favorite things about <i>Buffy</i>; there's nothing more rewarding for a viewer/reader than a story that doesn't forget its past). The most notable one that gets picked up in this volume is the main character, Barbie, who was a minor character in The Doll's House. In that volume, we </span>
  <span id="freeText10944659504507569425" style="display:none">A Game of You, the 5th volume in the Sandman series, is my favorite so far. One thing I've noticed Gaiman is very good at is picking up little threads he dropped in previous stories and building on them (this was one of my favorite things about <i>Buffy</i>; there's nothing more rewarding for a viewer/reader than a story that doesn't forget its past). The most notable one that gets picked up in this volume is the main character, Barbie, who was a minor character in The Doll's House. In that volume, we were introduced to her dream world very briefly, and I was surprised (and pleased) to see it turned into something bigger and darker.<br /><br />I've also noticed by this point that the story doesn't suffer when Morpheus takes a back seat to the action. Gaiman is skilled enough to provide a compelling cast instead of just relying on one strong character with an entourage of half-developed sidekicks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10944659504507569425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3110950021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15259368453061094537">In this fifth <i>Sandman</i> volume we meet some characters from previous ones. Barbie chief amongst them. She no longer dreams and as it turns out, that really will drive you mad - of sorts. We therefore get a magical dreamland, some colourful house-mates of different sexualities and *clears throat* ages and, of course, the Lord of Dreams.<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588365154i/29393392._SY540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Not sure if this volume actually does anything for the overall story - I'll only be able to judge that later - but it was quite some fun. From small-minded small-tow</span>
  <span id="freeText15259368453061094537" style="display:none">In this fifth <i>Sandman</i> volume we meet some characters from previous ones. Barbie chief amongst them. She no longer dreams and as it turns out, that really will drive you mad - of sorts. We therefore get a magical dreamland, some colourful house-mates of different sexualities and *clears throat* ages and, of course, the Lord of Dreams.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588365154i/29393392._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Not sure if this volume actually does anything for the overall story - I'll only be able to judge that later - but it was quite some fun. From small-minded small-town folks getting a nasty mirror held up to their faces (not that they'll actually be ashamed and change) to witches, hurricanes and talking animals, the authors managed to pack this rainbow-coloured story full of some deep musings about sexual identities, different kinds of childhoods, forgiveness and much more. *<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588365154i/29393393._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The most prominent topic in this volume was, once again, stories. How we make them, what they make us become in turn, the fact that we all have them.<br><br>I think my favourite character was Thessaly. No, she can't compete with Death, but she's close. And then there was one of THE most iconic images from the entire series (it's also one of the most beautiful in the series so far if you ask me):<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1588365154i/29393394._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>So as you can see, the art will probably always be this way and nothing too pretty, but in many instances that actually works well with the story - the story is eccentric and so is the art.<br><br>Not as powerful as some of the other volumes I've read so far but on the level these writers and artists are operating, that still means it's very good.<br><br><br>* <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I really am not the forgiving type. <div id="review_646337516" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/646337516" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/646337516?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer646337516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11768670590966680969"><br/>The Sandman series goes from strength to strength, capable of reinventing itself with each new collection. Morpheus is mostly absent at the start of <b> A Game of You </b> , but that's all right, as the new characters prove interesting enough without his presence. The story takes place partly in a New York tenement, peopled with oddball characters that for some reason reminded me of Will Eisner, and partly in a fantasy realm that is lost in a far corner of Morpheus' Dreamcountry. Connecting the two is </span>
  <span id="freeText11768670590966680969" style="display:none"><br />The Sandman series goes from strength to strength, capable of reinventing itself with each new collection. Morpheus is mostly absent at the start of <b> A Game of You </b> , but that's all right, as the new characters prove interesting enough without his presence. The story takes place partly in a New York tenement, peopled with oddball characters that for some reason reminded me of Will Eisner, and partly in a fantasy realm that is lost in a far corner of Morpheus' Dreamcountry. Connecting the two is Barbara, a character that appeared first in <b> Doll's House </b> where she dreamt she was a princess ruling over a magic realm. The Barbie connection was clearly stated from the start, and here it takes a turn for the dark side, in both realms. Barbie is divorced from Ken and living rather poorly in the big city, while the realm is under attack from the Cuckoo and her trusty courtiers are pleading for her return to save them from the Cuckoo. <br /><br />According to Wikipedia this story arc is one of the author's favorites, and I can see how some of his favorite themes of falling through the cracks of reality feature prominently here. Gaiman also mentions this is one of the 'girl' focused collections, as opposed to other 'male' oriented ones. He might have a point, but I didn't have any problems with following a mostly feminine cast. I would say that the appeal of the cast for me is in their marginalized social position: they are the misfits, the non-conformists that 'proper' people like to look down their noses and frown at. Wanda is a pre-operative shemale, Hazel and Foxglove are a couple of lesbians, Thessaly is a bespectacled bookworm that hides a long career in witchcraft, and so on. More than making a statement about social acceptance and exotic lifestyles, I think Gaiman chose these borderline characters in order to show how adversity can help show humans in their true light and how they can get out of trouble by sticking together and helping each other out.<br /><br />Not to get into too much details about the plot, but Barbie gets trapped in a dream by a servant of the Cuckoo, and her neighbours summon the Moon Goddess to help them follow up and rescue her, only to provoke a cataclysm into the real world. In the <b> Land </b> Barbie as Princess Barbara sets out on a classic fantasy quest through deserts and forest until she reaches her former palace and comes face to face with the Cuckoo. Morpheus at last makes an appearance and provides not only a godlike solution to everybody's troubles, but also a meditation on personal responsibility and assuming the consequences (bad or good) of one's actions, on growing up and leaving childhood dreams behind and fitting in in an adult world. I had some problems with decoding the role of the Cuckoo as an agent of chaos, but probably a re-read will set me straight.<br /><br />Of particular interest for me is the artwork of Shawn McManus, which I considered a step above previous volumes. I hope some of the characters here will return in later issues, particularly Martin Tenbones, if possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11768670590966680969" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2915937298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2915937298" />
    <a title="Zachary F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22548315-zachary-f"><img alt="Zachary F." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593017877p2/22548315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2915937298?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zachary F." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zachary F." href="/user/show/22548315-zachary-f">Zachary F.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=to-love-the-unreal">to-love-the-unreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=things-in-heaven-and-earth">things-in-heaven-and-earth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=gender-on-my-mind">gender-on-my-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=1950-to-1999">1950-to-1999</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548315-zachary-f?shelf=lgbtq-themes">lgbtq-themes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2915937298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9859668087997819927"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1565365676i/27963053.jpg' width='250' height='400' alt='Wanda' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I'll just launch right into it. <i>A Game of You</i> centers on a quintet of (mostly) mortal women sharing an apartment building in New York, two of whom are lesbians, one of whom is trans, one of whom is cishet, and the last of whom is an ageless witch. The cishet woman, Barbie, is the protagonist, or at least the person most of the action centers on; the trans woman, Wanda, is arguably the emotional core. For those not already in the loop, I'll also remind you that our author is a cishet man, and fur</span>
  <span id="freeText9859668087997819927" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1565365676i/27963053.jpg" width="250" height="400" alt="Wanda" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'll just launch right into it. <i>A Game of You</i> centers on a quintet of (mostly) mortal women sharing an apartment building in New York, two of whom are lesbians, one of whom is trans, one of whom is cishet, and the last of whom is an ageless witch. The cishet woman, Barbie, is the protagonist, or at least the person most of the action centers on; the trans woman, Wanda, is arguably the emotional core. For those not already in the loop, I'll also remind you that our author is a cishet man, and furthermore that this was written in the early '90s. Probably you're beginning to see the potential complications.<br><br>Let me acknowledge right off the bat that Gaiman clearly has good intentions. Each of these women (with one understandable exception) is portrayed sympathetically and affectionately, and it's pretty obvious our author's goal is to provide positive and affirmative queer representation in a period when that would still have been rare from a mainstream creator not directly affiliated with the LGBTQ community. I've read commentary from several—though certainly not all—trans readers who say Wanda was one of the first relatable transgender characters they came across in popular media, and that for that reason they're willing to overlook any authorial missteps. In short, Neil's heart is in the right place, and I think it (mostly) shows.<br><br>That said, boy is this a cringe-inducing read in 2019. We run pretty much the whole gamut of well-intended but ill-informed trans character tropes, from the fixation on Wanda's dead name and genitals (she spends pretty much the whole book in lacy underwear) to the biological essentialism of even the supernatural characters (the gods themselves, apparently, can't quite comprehend a woman who doesn't come equipped with the "proper" hardware) and ultimately to her <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[heroic and moving—but nonetheless inevitable—death <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <a href="https://www.themarysue.com/trans-characters-in-comics/" rel="nofollow noopener">This article</a> breaks down the problematic stuff in more detail, if you’re interested.<br><br>I think the real issue here is that, instead of writing a story which happens to involve a trans character in a leading role, Gaiman decided to write a story which is in large part about <i>being trans</i>. And when you set out to write a story about <i>being</i> something, especially something you yourself are not, it's crucial to tread carefully and to make absolutely sure you know what you're talking about. Maybe <i>A Game of You</i> is the best that could be expected given the period and the author, maybe not; but books live and age like human beings, and it's both permissible and important to remain in dialogue with them, to remember the relevant context without wielding it as a catch-all excuse for their flaws. (It's worth noting that Gaiman himself <a href="https://twitter.com/neilhimself/status/996455063135629312" rel="nofollow noopener">has said</a> he probably wouldn't write this story today.)<br><br>Even without the gender trouble, though, this arc didn't impress me much. We spend a large part of it in Barbie's personal dreamscape, a fantasy kindom with deliberate shades of Tolkien, Lewis, and Carroll, trying to track down an elusive nemesis called the Cuckoo. I never found the threat to be particularly well-developed, and the resolution—a typical <i>Sandman</i> deus ex machina, of the kind I complained about in my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2905131601" rel="nofollow noopener">last review</a>—was no more satisfying. The epilogue sequence at <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Wanda's funeral <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> was slightly more so, though of course it also plays into a number of the cringey clichés mentioned above—including, y'know, being set at <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a trans woman's untimely funeral <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>So. A dispensable volume at best, and at worst an actively offensive one. Other readers may and do disagree about <i>A Game of You</i>'s relative merits, but for my own part I can see little reason to recommend it when we've got plenty of much better trans narratives <i>and</i> much better <i>Sandman</i> stories out there to choose from.<br><br>----<br>See my reviews for the whole <i>Sandman</i> series here:<br><br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2892445761" rel="nofollow noopener">Preludes and Nocturnes</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2893930178" rel="nofollow noopener">The Doll's House</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2901248091" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream Country</a>
</i> (★★★☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2905131601" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Mists</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>A Game of You</i> (★★★☆☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2909716308" rel="nofollow noopener">Fables and Reflections</a>
</i> (★★★★☆)<br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2924339790" rel="nofollow noopener">Brief Lives</a>

<div id="review_245315829" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/245315829" />
    <a title="Anthony Chavez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4876333-anthony-chavez"><img alt="Anthony Chavez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314570438p2/4876333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245315829?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony Chavez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony Chavez" href="/user/show/4876333-anthony-chavez">Anthony Chavez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4876333-anthony-chavez?shelf=graphic-novel-manga">graphic-novel-manga</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer245315829&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245315829" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9433367781142096921">"It's like we fell down the rabbit hole, woke up in... I don't know. Stephen King's basement..." (Wanda to George's head) (91) <br/><br/>I love how Gaiman brings back former characters, and is able to intertwine them all. The overarching story has also moved along, ever so slightly. The last three volumes of The Sandman have completely outdone what came before. I thought there was a good story in the first two volumes, but it was hard to find what really made me keep reading. I'm glad I kept reading thoug</span>
  <span id="freeText9433367781142096921" style="display:none">"It's like we fell down the rabbit hole, woke up in... I don't know. Stephen King's basement..." (Wanda to George's head) (91) <br /><br />I love how Gaiman brings back former characters, and is able to intertwine them all. The overarching story has also moved along, ever so slightly. The last three volumes of The Sandman have completely outdone what came before. I thought there was a good story in the first two volumes, but it was hard to find what really made me keep reading. I'm glad I kept reading though, because Gaiman really came into his own in volumes 3-5, and the fifth volume, "A Game of You," is easily the best written installment so far, and I think I said that about the last two volumes as I read them.<br /><br />While the Sandman himself hardly appears in this volume, he didn't really need to be and it did not draw away from the story as a whole. Gaiman has begun to write from character, and that really helps the story move forward in unpredictable but great ways. It's about a girl who dreams herself into a fantasy realm that is endangered by "the Cuckoo," who sends agents into the real world to have her and her friends killed. The story alternates between New York and "The Land," and it's so well done that you won't even wonder where Morpheus is. <br /><br />Not only is this a great story, it also functions in tying the previous volumes together. The main character is Barbie, who was part of the goody-goody couple Barbie and Ken in Vol. 2: The Doll's House; furthermore, Foxglove is actually the lover of the lesbian woman at the diner from Vol. 1: Preludes and Nocturnes. It's a very tight story, and in some ways is a good sequel to "The Doll's House." <br /><br />Now I see what people were talking about when they gushed about this series. I really enjoyed this, from start to end, and have loved every volume. Little things like how Hazel and Foxglove weren't the most perfect couple, but they were able to work out their differences, that was nice. I liked how Wanda's family wasn't so accepting, but Maisie, the crazy homeless lady, is able to. I liked the characters, and I liked seeing Barbie changed and change more throughout. <br /><br />"But surely you want to go home," (Wanda's aunt to Barbara)<br />"I'm... I'm not sure about that. I don't think home's a place. It's a state of mind." (177) </span>
  <a data-text-id="9433367781142096921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1104958077" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1104958077" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1104958077?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781417686148" href="/book/show/1023047.The_Sandman_5">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=gn-superhero-scifi-fantasy">gn-superhero-scifi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1104958077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4604822694536858555">This is a great volume focused less on Dream/Morpheus himself than on dream worlds populated by Narnia-like and stuffed animal characters from Barbie's youth, "morphed" into somewhat scarier beings, this being Gaiman, and the dreams of her friends Wanda, a drag Queen, a lesbian couple, a witch named Thessaly, a "street" person who hates dogs, and a scary monster named Cuckoo… and there's a couple gruesome moments in it that just may stay with you… but what also may stay with you is the endorseme</span>
  <span id="freeText4604822694536858555" style="display:none">This is a great volume focused less on Dream/Morpheus himself than on dream worlds populated by Narnia-like and stuffed animal characters from Barbie's youth, "morphed" into somewhat scarier beings, this being Gaiman, and the dreams of her friends Wanda, a drag Queen, a lesbian couple, a witch named Thessaly, a "street" person who hates dogs, and a scary monster named Cuckoo… and there's a couple gruesome moments in it that just may stay with you… but what also may stay with you is the endorsement it makes for dreams/fantasy/imagination/story (and as with Gaiman, always, the importance of horror/fear in the construction of "reality". Dream and Death play small but memorable roles in this one, where Gaiman allows what he has to say to play out through his other quite memorable characters. This is a feminist tale, told primarily with and about a range of women; maybe the gender/sexuality "point" seems like it is being pushed a little hard in places, but in general it plays out beautifully and interestingly.<br /><br />And the artwork. Have I said anything before about the covers, by Dave McKean? They are fabulous, really unsettling and amazing as a foundation for the tales. Some of the artwork (mainly by Shawn McManus, but some by Colleen Doran) seems younger and more colorful to fit Barbie's child fantasy/dream world, but even acknowledging that, I don't like it as much as some of the earlier or later artwork. Nevretheless, McKean's deeper, darker themes undergird the larger project, give it resonance. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4604822694536858555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_838031647" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/838031647" />
    <a title="Selkie ✦ Queen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen"><img alt="Selkie ✦ Queen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587803822p2/28165536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/838031647?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Selkie ✦ Queen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Selkie ✦ Queen" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen">Selkie ✦ Queen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781563890895" href="/book/show/471899.A_Game_of_You">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=my-reviews">my-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=graphic-novel">graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=vertigo-comics">vertigo-comics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer838031647" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2837419660325344212">In the next ten months of reading all of its volumes or so, I didn't really think it was possible for me to fall in love with <i>The Sandman</i>. I knew I was already a fan since <i>The Doll's House</i>, but my appreciation and affection for Gaiman's work were not as intense or as consuming as I initially thought they would become sooner of later (and that happened later on once I got my hands on Gaiman's collaborative volume with Jill Thompson, but I digress).<br/><br/>I enjoyed what the previous volume <i>Season of Mist</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2837419660325344212" style="display:none">In the next ten months of reading all of its volumes or so, I didn't really think it was possible for me to fall in love with <i>The Sandman</i>. I knew I was already a fan since <i>The Doll's House</i>, but my appreciation and affection for Gaiman's work were not as intense or as consuming as I initially thought they would become sooner of later (and that happened later on once I got my hands on Gaiman's collaborative volume with Jill Thompson, but I digress).<br /><br />I enjoyed what the previous volume <i>Season of Mists</i> had to offer. It was spectacular in scope, touching upon old faiths and religions and the complex interpretation of Lucifer Morningstar, as well as Dream's long overdue resolution with his former lover, Nada. My interest for this series of graphic novels was then maintained and I looked forward to what was in store.<br /><br />What I got in <i>A Game of You</i> was initially disheartening, only because I was once again thrown off balance from the major plot toward a self-contained narrative which the fifth volume was all about. I wasn't entirely happy about the sudden shift from volume 2 <i>The Doll's House</i> to volume 3 <i>Dream Country</i> (an anthology of short stories about secondary characters), so I was just as displeased when after <i>Season of Mists</i>, I had to contend myself with yet another separate events from the major storyline I'm already invested in (where Dream is the focus). Nevertheless, <i>A Game of You</i> proved to be a daring story centered on women from different walks of life: Barbie (whom we previously encountered in the second volume); the lesbian couple Hazel and Foxglove, the transgendered man Wanda/Alvin and the mysterious college student Thessaly. <br /><br />The concept of <i>Game</i> was written with a feminist perspective; Gaiman brought forth these conflicted women into the dreamscape to expound on the difficulties they faced both as social beings in the context of the unbelievable pressures the female gender has always faced; and as visitors/invaders of the Dreaming where they are thrust into an unknown territory that could change their lives forever. It is worth noting that Wanda was the only one left behind while the other four women were able to travel (and it's for the simple fact that he is physiologically not a woman at all). <br /><br />Gaiman highlighted the long-held mysticism associated with female consciousness such as the connection pertaining to women's blood cycle and the phases of the moon; and how Barbie's heightened sense of fantasy has allowed her to bridge two worlds together. Queer relationships were touched upon with Hazel and Foxglove whose primary conflict was the unexpected pregnancy of the other due to a drunken mistake; and how it would affect the direction of their partnership. Thessaly, a later important character in the series, is a self-made woman who acts according to her survival instincts, if not because of the blatant disregard for the welfare of others she considers to be inferior to her. However, she is the most engaging character in the volume, and was able to capture the interest of Dream himself when he later arrives in the story to resolve the complications these women caught themselves in.<br /><br />It's easy to dismiss the eventual arrival of Dream as a deus ex machina wrap-up that is anti-climactic, but after several readings of the entire volume, I realized that it was the most effective pay-off for the story. Barbie claims herself from the broken fragments of her dreamland and returns home to grieve the loss of her childhood and an unlikely friend. Thessaly once again defines herself outside the safety of societal expectations, and Hazel and Foxglove learn to build their relationship with more trust and open communication. The story of Wanda/Alvin is also very stirring and emotional, and its conclusion is one of the most unforgettable moments in the series for me.<br /><br />Dream, on the other hand, says goodbye to another lover, and warns the women that they must take heed not to endanger themselves by their own hand because the only thing that truly oppresses them is the fact that they have learned to love their bondage. He was also beginning to soften a bit, thanks to the constant interactions with human beings and with the arrival of his secret admirer, the faery Nuala who was first introduced in <i>Season of Mists</i>.<br /><br />After being able to refresh all of these moments in the book, <i>A Game of You</i> eventually became one of my top favorites of the series. Every time I read this volume, there's always something new to ponder within myself and discuss with people.<br /><br /><b>RECOMMENDED: 10/10</b><br /><i>* This volume is an acquired taste, however</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="2837419660325344212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1078525273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1078525273?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=gender-sex-feminism">gender-sex-feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=comics">comics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1078525273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11117502900641620061">Example #832 as to why cis people should never ever attempt to write about trans women. Ever. Every single negative stereotype is included here, from the age-old trope of "the surgery" and depictions of trans women as frivolous and overemotional to the extreme and wrapping up with the "trans woman as tragic warning" trope that means we can only ever be killed and even then denied the very identity we died to achieve. And so much misgendering. So much. From the common "oh, Wanda's really a man" t</span>
  <span id="freeText11117502900641620061" style="display:none">Example #832 as to why cis people should never ever attempt to write about trans women. Ever. Every single negative stereotype is included here, from the age-old trope of "the surgery" and depictions of trans women as frivolous and overemotional to the extreme and wrapping up with the "trans woman as tragic warning" trope that means we can only ever be killed and even then denied the very identity we died to achieve. And so much misgendering. So much. From the common "oh, Wanda's really a man" to a rather imaginative new form of exclusion with "I'm sorry, but the moon can't take you to dream land because only <i>real</i> women can use her magic."<br /><br />I wish I weren't a completeist. I wish I'd have just skipped this entire volume beforehand, because then my love for the series would remain untarnished and I'd not have to add Neil Gaiman to the list of writers I adore but I can't think of without cringing. I picked up this graphic novel because I wanted to relax in the brisk autumn afternoon and think on subjects that were far from my daily life. To see my life written as a tragicomic mockery that reinforces so many harmful concepts was not what I was in the mood for. At all.</span>

<div id="review_1651612446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1651612446" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1651612446?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18912169-a-game-of-you">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dark-fantasy-gothic">dark-fantasy-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=horror-occult">horror-occult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1651612446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9720862719888886421"><b>Sandman, Vol 5: A Game of You: Challenges our childhood fantasies</b><br/><br/>After the excellent Vol 4: Season of Mists, the Sandman once again retreats to the shadows in this unified story arc. Instead it focuses on Barbie, the vapid-seeming blonde who was married to Ken and lived in the same house as Rose Walker in Vol 2: The Doll’s House. She has since split with Ken and moved to NY. She lives in a building with several unusual characters: Wanda, a pre-operative transsexual woman; a mysterious and timid-</span>
  <span id="freeText9720862719888886421" style="display:none"><b>Sandman, Vol 5: A Game of You: Challenges our childhood fantasies</b><br /><br />After the excellent Vol 4: Season of Mists, the Sandman once again retreats to the shadows in this unified story arc. Instead it focuses on Barbie, the vapid-seeming blonde who was married to Ken and lived in the same house as Rose Walker in Vol 2: The Doll’s House. She has since split with Ken and moved to NY. She lives in a building with several unusual characters: Wanda, a pre-operative transsexual woman; a mysterious and timid-seeming girl named Thessaly; a lesbian couple named Hazel and Foxglove; and a creepy guy named George. All these people share two things - they are marginalized members of society, and they also hide various secrets beneath their surfaces.<br /><br />One of the big themes in the story revolves around Wanda, as she has consciously sought to discard her identity as Alvin, growing up in an intolerant small town in Kansas. Wanda is an upbeat, supportive friend to Barbara, and in typical New York fashion, they are regularly broke but try to look out for each other. The story is clearly sympathetic to Wanda’s attempt to make the physical changes that will allow her to be fully female, and shows the prejudices she faced growing up when Barbie visits her hometown in Kansas. As a reader I’m also sympathetic to her plight, but I wasn’t sure how this fit in with the largest context of the SANDMAN series.<br /><br />I won’t summarize the plot details, other than to say that the story has a lot of horror elements and some really gruesome moments involving nightmares. Barbie and her friends are drawn into a quest to save some fantasy creatures in a place called the Land, which is being menaced by a sinister creature called the Cuckoo. This fantasy world intrudes into our real world, and overlaps slightly with Morpheus’ world of the Dreaming. But it is largely self-contained.<br /><br />This volume is all about the fantasy worlds we create in our minds, and the purposes they serve. It is also about appearances and how deceptive they are. Finally, and most punishing to the reader, it deconstructs one such fantasy world, the Land that Barbie has visited in her dreams since childhood, in a very cruel manner. I was initially unsure exactly what I didn’t really like about this volume (and later read that for many readers this is also their least favorite Sandman story), but after some reflection I think it was this aspect of destroying childhood fantasies and suggesting that they need to be dissolved in order to move on into adulthood. <br /><br />Neil Gaiman is a master of storytelling, mythology, dreams, horror, and the fantastic, so having Barbie’s world attacked by the villainous Cuckoo was surprising. Fantasy stories certainly have strong elements of escapism, especially epic fantasy tales, and although the urban fantasies of Gaiman are less so, they all suggest fantastic elements lurking beneath the mundane and drab details of the ‘real’ world. So I wasn’t expecting him to essentially go after such fantasies is such ruthless fashion. <br /><br />Is it really so childish and wrong to create fantasy worlds in our heads, often to escape from unpleasant reality? If so, does that not negate much of the genre’s worth? Or did I just misunderstand the message of this volume. Either way, it didn’t seem to mesh well with the other volumes, though I admire Gaiman’s willingness to explore a range of themes and be unafraid to turn off fans in some cases. That is the mark of a true storyteller - one that does not simply cater to our expectations, but challenges them instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9720862719888886421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

However, I liked reading Barbie’s story even if Dream didn’t appear much in this volume.   <span id="freeText5771767559792068885" style="display:none">After reading issues 29-31 and not enjoying them at all, I expected this volume to be not so good.         rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3513388671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12832302324260976788">Sandman gets better and better as we return to Barbie, a character from Book 2.<br/><br/>This time Barbie is trying to live her life the way she wants. With her best friend she travels the streets of New York being free. However, the Cuckoo wants revenge and sends its creatures to try and get Barbie back into dream land. In doing so Barbie and her whole building of neighbors have to step into the dream world again to survive.<br/><br/>This is basically a rescue mission but with a lot of interesting twist and turns.</span>
  <span id="freeText12832302324260976788" style="display:none">Sandman gets better and better as we return to Barbie, a character from Book 2.<br /><br />This time Barbie is trying to live her life the way she wants. With her best friend she travels the streets of New York being free. However, the Cuckoo wants revenge and sends its creatures to try and get Barbie back into dream land. In doing so Barbie and her whole building of neighbors have to step into the dream world again to survive.<br /><br />This is basically a rescue mission but with a lot of interesting twist and turns. People seem shocked Sandman is almost a background character but a almighty god is only so interesting, it's better he's the surrounding the main characters so he gives a bigger impression of "super" powered. I also really enjoyed the supporting cast. From two lesbian characters who are figuring out their relationship, a trans character who will do anything to save a friend, and a witch, everyone has time to show off their skills and background.<br /><br />Sandman is a slow burn. There's not major fights or big climatic showdowns. Despite being in the DC universe, it doesn't feel anything like a DC book. It took me years to read this. In fact I read volume 1 around age 16 and didn't like it much. At 31 I appreciate the slow burn storytelling and deep look into multiple character's lives. This was a fantastic volume with a somber but touching ending. A 5 out of 5.</span>

<div id="review_2957066879" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2957066879" />
    <a title="Sud666" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666"><img alt="Sud666" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418437112p2/36890366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2957066879?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sud666" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sud666" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666">Sud666</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=comics">comics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2957066879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4385261722281776900">Volume 5 of the Sandman series was called "A Game of You". It tells the story of Barbie, whom we first saw back in vol 2 "The Doll's House", and her dreams. Her dreams have created an entire world, but this world has been taken over by a malevolent creature known as the "Cuckoo".<br/><br/>Interestingly each of the individual issues is named after a song. From "Lullaby of Broadway", "Bad Moon Rising", "Beginning to See the Light", "Over the Sea to Sky" and "I Woke Up and One of Us Was Crying".<br/><br/>The central c</span>
  <span id="freeText4385261722281776900" style="display:none">Volume 5 of the Sandman series was called "A Game of You". It tells the story of Barbie, whom we first saw back in vol 2 "The Doll's House", and her dreams. Her dreams have created an entire world, but this world has been taken over by a malevolent creature known as the "Cuckoo".<br /><br />Interestingly each of the individual issues is named after a song. From "Lullaby of Broadway", "Bad Moon Rising", "Beginning to See the Light", "Over the Sea to Sky" and "I Woke Up and One of Us Was Crying".<br /><br />The central character of Barbie was really a minor player in the Vol 2 Dolls House, she lived in the same apt that Rose Walker lived in and her dreams gave rise to another world. But, Barbie no longer dreams. She lives in an apt building with some neighbors she is close with- Wanda (a transvestite), Hazel and Foxglove (a lesbian couple), Thessaly (a powerful witch), and George who seems to be a quiet person.<br /><br />She chooses to leave the waking world by using the Porpentine, a magical amulet and goes to her world in the dreaming. She finds it has been taken over by the Cuckoo. She joins in a quest to defeat the Cuckoo, who retaliates by taking over George and using him to attack the residents of the building by giving them bad dreams. This causes Thessaly to kill George and find out what's going on- then she, Hazel and Foxglove enter the dream world to try to find Barbie.<br /><br />Barbie's adventures in the dream world are numerous and she faces not only bravery but also treachery. It ends with the events leading to Morpheus appearing to "fix" things. Without being too spoilery that is the gist.<br /><br />A Game of You was an interesting story. It certainly has some good original concepts, but the character of Barbie and her erstwhile companions did not really resonate (though I thought Thessaly the witch was a great character) and the entire issue just seemed to be lacking. The ending, in regards to Morpheus' actions, I actually appreciated. The end of Wanda's story was also poignant.<br /><br />But, it was not really as exciting and interesting as the other volumes. By no means is this to be considered a bad volume but in comparison to the quality of the rest of the series. Any Gaiman fan will appreciate this, while nonfans may be left with a WTF feeling. Not the best of the Sandman volumes, but still worth reading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4385261722281776900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11564987358857788100"><i>"It's like, that people... well, that everybody has a secret world inside of them. I mean everybody, all of the people in the whole world -- no matter how dull and boring they are on the outside. Inside them they've all got unimaginable, wonderful, stupid, amazing worlds... not just one world. Hundreds of them. Thousands, maybe."</i><br/><br/>This volume is pretty much a self-contained story, with no flashing to different time periods with different characters or inserting separate stories in-between another </span>
  <span id="freeText11564987358857788100" style="display:none"><i>"It's like, that people... well, that everybody has a secret world inside of them. I mean everybody, all of the people in the whole world -- no matter how dull and boring they are on the outside. Inside them they've all got unimaginable, wonderful, stupid, amazing worlds... not just one world. Hundreds of them. Thousands, maybe."</i><br><br>This volume is pretty much a self-contained story, with no flashing to different time periods with different characters or inserting separate stories in-between another chapter. I appreciated that - and the story here was really good too. It's basically about this small group of women who live in the same apartment building and end up having to go to one of the dream lands to help their friend Barbara, who has gone into a coma (and was briefly introduced in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25099.The_Sandman__Vol__2_The_Doll_s_House__The_Sandman__2_" title="The Sandman, Vol. 2 The Doll's House (The Sandman #2) by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 2: The Doll's House</a>), fight a villain called the cuckoo. It's got the usual Gaiman-esque horror and occult features - and other types of general weirdness. <br><br>I think this volume is one of my favorites so far. I really liked Barbara's dream land friends.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1434866243i/15273098._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> </span>
